<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="59c08588d08e450b85e7b8bfbf4e2444"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="59c08588d08e450b85e7b8bfbf4e2444">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '0347c24e-075b-4c07-99fd-5d50a541be4a'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '95C47B81BD513AAB87C7D9FFE2E7422A';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;

                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d170554624" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Exploiting high-performance yet low-cost hard carbon anodes is crucial to advancing the state-of-the-art sodium-ion batteries. However, the achievement of superior initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) and high Na-storage capacity via low-temperature carbonization remains challenging due to the presence of tremendous defects with few closed pores. Here, a facile hybrid carbon framework design is proposed from the polystyrene precursor bearing distinct molecular bridges at a low pyrolysis temperature of 800C via in situ fusion and embedding strategy. This is realized by integrating triazine- and carbonyl-crosslinked polystyrene nanospheres during carbonization. The triazine crosslinking allows in situ fusion of spheres into layered carbon with low defects&nbsp;and abundant closed pores, which serves as a matrix for embedding the well-retained carbon spheres with nanopores/defects derived from carbonyl crosslinking. Therefore, the hybrid hard carbon with intimate interface showcases synergistic Na ions storage behavior, showing an ICE of 70.2%, a high capacity of 279.3mAhg<sup>1</sup>, and long-term 500 cycles, superior to carbons from the respective precursor and other reported carbons fabricated under the low carbonization temperature. The present protocol opens new avenues toward low-cost hard carbon anode materials for high-performance sodium-ion batteries.</p></div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2479-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2479-sec-0010-title">1 INTRODUCTION</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been regarded as one of the most promising candidates for large-scale energy storage systems to support sustainable energy from renewable sources due to their low cost and inexhaustible sodium resources.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0001_R_d170554616e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem Soc Rev">1</a>-<a href="#cey2479-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0003_R_d170554616e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - eScience">3</a></sup></span> Unfortunately, commercialized graphite anodes in lithium-ion batteries have been proven problematic for storing Na ions, due to their larger radius and unfavorable thermodynamics.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0004_R_d170554616e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - New Carbon Mater">4</a>-<a href="#cey2479-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0006_R_d170554616e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Storage Mater">6</a></sup></span> Therefore, searching for reliable anode materials for SIBs in practical applications is highly desirable and urgent.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0007_R_d170554616e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv Energy Mater">7</a>-<a href="#cey2479-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0011_R_d170554616e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Small Methods">11</a></sup></span> Hard carbons, composed of randomly stacked graphitic microcrystalline layers, feature unique structures of abundant open/closed nanopores, defect sites, and enlarged layer spacing. Consequently, Na storage in hard carbons can be accomplished by pore surface adsorption, defect combination, interlayer intercalation and nanocluster formation, thus exhibiting mainly a combined high-potential slope and low-potential plateau curve with a relatively large capacity (300mAhg<sup>1</sup>). Hence, hard carbons have been considered as one of the most promising anode materials for SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0012_R_d170554616e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - SusMat">12</a>-<a href="#cey2479-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0015_R_d170554616e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Carbon Energy">15</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To date, tremendous efforts have been devoted to designing nanoarchitectures of hard carbons for fast kinetics with improved rate and cyclic performances.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0016_R_d170554616e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv Energy Mater">16</a>, <a href="#cey2479-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0017_R_d170554616e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Carbon">17</a></sup></span> For example, nanospheres, nanotubes, and hierarchical porous structures have been designed to enhance cyclic and rate performances by increasing active sites and shortening ion transport pathways.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0018_R_d170554616e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv Energy Mater">18</a>, <a href="#cey2479-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0019_R_d170554616e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv Energy Mater">19</a></sup></span> However, the presence of abundant defects or micropores inevitably induces excessive electrolyte decomposition to form unstable solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layers, resulting in a high irreversible capacity with insufficient initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE). For instance, typical ICEs for those nanostructured carbons are limited, such as&nbsp;carbon nanospheres, carbon nanotubes, and hierarchically ordered porous carbons showing 41.5%,<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0016_R_d170554616e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv Energy Mater">16</a></sup></span> 41.8%,<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0017_R_d170554616e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Carbon">17</a></sup></span> and 26.7%,<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0018_R_d170554616e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv Energy Mater">18</a></sup></span> respectively. Such low ICE in anodes will lead to the consumption of excessive Na ions from Na-containing cathode materials in a full cell, thus greatly reducing the energy density of the full cell and hindering practical applications. Therefore, a high ICE of hard carbon anodes is extremely desirable for Na storage.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0020_R_d170554616e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv Energy Mater">20</a>-<a href="#cey2479-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0022_R_d170554616e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Energy">22</a></sup></span> Explorations have shown that reducing the specific surface area of hard carbons is favorable to improving ICE by reducing the irreversible reaction caused by micropores, defects, and so on.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0023_R_d170554616e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv Energy Mater">23</a></sup></span> For instance, Sun et al.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0024_R_d170554616e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J Mater Chem A">24</a></sup></span> synthesized biomass-derived honeycomb-like hard carbon with a specific BrunauerEmmettTeller surface area (<i>S</i><sub>BET</sub>) of 82.8m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup> at 1200C, delivering an enhanced ICE of 67.7%. Carbon nanofibers with a low <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> of 20m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup> can be achieved by carbonization above 2000C, exhibiting a reversible capacity of 200mAhg<sup>1</sup> with ICE of 68%.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0025_R_d170554616e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv Energy Mater">25</a></sup></span> It can be seen that higher carbonization temperature generally help to decrease <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> for most hard carbons (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>), which is beneficial to boosting ICE to a certain extent. Meanwhile, with the increase of carbonization temperature, the content of closed pores increases, which is also favorable for large plateau capacity and ICE.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0026_R_d170554616e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv Funct Mater">26</a>, <a href="#cey2479-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0027_R_d170554616e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv Energy Mater">27</a></sup></span> However, the high-temperature treatment unavoidably raises costs with high energy consumption, and meanwhile results in concurrent changes in electrical conductivities, defect densities, surface chemistry, framework microstructures, and so forth, giving rise to the difficulty in identifying the structureproperty relationship. Additionally, the carbon framework characteristics are also dictated by their precursors, and thus distinct precursors further make the reliable and systematic structureproperty investigations difficult. In our previous work, the low-porosity carbon was prepared by coupling benzene rings of polystyrene by Scholl reaction followed by carbonization at a low temperature of 800C.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0028_R_d170554616e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Carbon">28</a></sup></span> The coupled benzene rings can promote the aromatization of polymers during pyrolysis, which was beneficial to the growth of pseudographitic phases and contributed to an enhanced ICE. Nevertheless, such crosslinking results in the nanomorphology collapse upon carbonization, restricting the fast ion transport and thus poor rate and cycling performances. Therefore, preparing hybrid hard carbons (HHCs) combined with low defects, abundant close pores, and well-defined nanostructures at low temperatures is anticipated to holistically promote Na storage, which has still been scarcely explored.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we propose the design of HHC with low surface area, abundant closed pores, and embedded nanospherical morphology from similar polystyrene precursors at a low temperature of 800C. During the carbonization process, the triazine-crosslinked polystyrene (TZ-PS) nanospheres undergo in situ fusion forming a continuous layered matrix with low surface area and abundant closed pores (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0001">1A</a>), whereas the carbonyl-crosslinked polystyrene (CO-PS) nanospheres demonstrate well morphology-preserved spheres with high surface area and sufficient interlayer spacing (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0001">1B</a>). Consequently, the cocarbonization of both hypercrosslinked polystyrenes leads to the formation of the low surface area HHCs with closed pores and defects/nanopores (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0001">1C</a>). This is different from previously reported hybrid carbons, which were generally produced by the carbonization of distinct precursors with large interfacial barrier and large domain size, whereas the present in situ fusing/embedding of polystyrene-based precursors can promote intimate contact/interaction within HHCs. As a result, the hybrid polystyrene-based carbon achieves excellent Na storage performances, including a higher ICE of 70.2% and a larger specific charge capacity of 279.3mAhg<sup>1</sup>, far exceeding 46.0% and 132.1mAhg<sup>1</sup> for CO-PS-derived carbon and 58.3% and 165.0mAhg<sup>1</sup> for TZ-PS-derived carbon. Meanwhile, the strategy can be extended to using waste plastic polystyrene for developing excellent-performance hard carbons, serving as a waste-to-wealth approach. Our finding here offers a viable and promising strategy for developing high-performance and low-cost hard carbons for Na storage and propels the implementation of SIBs technology.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cey2479-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a189899f-b104-4d97-b736-06f962442a3b/cey2479-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>

         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2479-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2479-sec-0020-title">2 EXPERIMENTAL SECTION</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2479-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2479-sec-0030-title">2.1 Preparation of PS&nbsp;nanospheres</h3>
            
            <p>Polystyrene nanospheres were synthesized according to microemulsion polymerization. Before use, the monomer styrene needs to remove the inhibitor through a column filled with alumina. Styrene and half of the total amount of divinylbenzene were added to deionized water in a three-necked flask purged with nitrogen. After stirring for 5min, potassium persulfate was added to initiate the polymerization at 75C. After 3h, the remaining divinylbenzene was added to the emulsion, and the polymerization was continued for another 24h. After the reaction, the resultant products were collected by centrifugation, washed with ethanol three times, and then dried at 60C. The amounts of deionized water, styrene, divinylbenzene, and potassium persulfate are 300mL, 7.5mL, 1.5mL, and 300mg, respectively.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2479-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2479-sec-0040-title">2.2 Preparation of hypercrosslinked PS</h3>
            
            <p>Both CC and CO crosslinkers between polystyrene chains were prepared using the FriedelCrafts alkylation reaction. The TZ-PSs was prepared using polystyrene nanospheres as the raw material, anhydrous AlCl<sub>3</sub> as the FriedelCrafts catalyst, dichloromethane as the solvent, and cyanuric chloride as the crosslinker. Polystyrene (0.5g) was dispersed in 50mL dichloromethane, followed by adding 0.74g of cyanuric chloride. After stirring at reflux temperature for 10min, 1.5g of anhydrous AlCl<sub>3</sub> was added to the above mixture which was, then, refluxed with magnetic stirring for 12h. After that, the reaction was terminated by adding acetonewater solution, and the product was filtered, washed with acetone and 1M HCl mixed solution (volume ratio of 3:1), and then dried at 60C for 24h. The CO-PSs was treated with the same synthetic method except that CCl<sub>4</sub> was used as as both crosslinker and solvent. The reaction was terminated by adding an ethanol and water solution, and the product was filtered, washed with ethanol and 1M HCl mixed solution (volume ratio of 3:1), and then dried at 60C for 24h. When using waste polystyrene plastic as raw materials, the preparation processes are similar.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2479-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2479-sec-0050-title">2.3 Preparation of hypercrosslinked PS-derived carbons</h3>
            
            <p>The as-prepared precursors were carbonized in a furnace at a designated temperature for 3h with a heating rate of 2Cmin<sup>1</sup>. HHC was obtained by mixing of TZ-PS and CO-PS homogeneously, followed by carbonization. HHC-1h and HHC-12h were prepared following the same method as HHC except that carbonization times were 1h and 12h, respectively.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2479-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2479-sec-0060-title">2.4 Structural characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The morphologies of samples were characterized using a field-emission scanning electron microscope (SEM; FEI NovaTM Nano SEM 450) and transmission electron microscope (Talos F200X). Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) was carried out on a TENSOR II spectrometer using potassium bromide pellets in transmission mode. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was employed via an STA 449F5 thermal analyzer from room temperature to 800C at a heating rate of 10Cmin<sup>1</sup> under argon flow. The porosity was examined using N<sub>2</sub> adsorptiondesorption isotherms on a Micromeritics ASAP 2020 at 77K, and the calculation of BET surface area was based on BET theory. Before the adsorptiondesorption measurement, the polymers and carbons were heat-treated at 120C and 250C, respectively, for 6h under vacuum to remove moisture trapped in the pores. The pore size distribution was analyzed by density functional theory using the adsorption data. The X-ray powder diffraction patterns were recorded on a D8 DISCOVER diffractometer using Cu K radiation (40kV, 30mA, 0.15406nm). Raman spectroscopy measurements were performed in a Renishaw inVia Raman spectroscope with a 532nm laser. True density data were measured by Ultrapyc 3000 using helium as analysis gas. The equation (<i>V</i><sub>Close pore</sub>=1/<i></i><sub>true</sub>1/2.26) was used to calculate the internal closed pore volume, as ideal graphite materials have closed-pore-free layers of compressed carbon with a maximum density of 2.26gcm<sup>3</sup>. Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) data were collected using&nbsp;the X-ray diffractometer in a transmission and parallel-beam geometry with a Ni-filtered Cu K radiation and a scintillation point detector (Xenocs Xeuss 2.0). Sample powder was filled in a borosilicate glass capillary and rotated during the measurement. The scattering vector is a function of <i>Q</i> in a typical SAXS spectrum. The size of the structural elements (such as pores and particles) that scatter the X-ray intensity increases with decreasing <i>Q</i> values.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2479-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2479-sec-0070-title">2.5 Na&nbsp;storage measurements</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The electrodes were fabricated by mixing the as-obtained carbon samples as active materials&nbsp;and a binder of polyacrylic acid at a weight ratio of 90:10, and then the slurry was coated uniformly on a copper foil. The mass loading of active materials was approximately 1mgcm<sup>2</sup>. The electrodes were dried at 80C under vacuum for at least 8h and then cut into a round disk with a diameter of 10mm. Further drying each disk was performed at 80C until the weight remained constant. The half-cell electrochemical measurements were carried out in 2032-type coin cells assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox with the above-prepraed electrode as working electrode, Na foil as the counter electrode, and 1M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme as the electrolyte solution within a potential range of 02V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) with a scan rate of 0.11.0mVs<sup>1</sup> and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were performed on an electrochemical workstation. In situ EIS profiles were collected under the combination of EIS and the galvanostatic dischargecharge processes with different potentials within a current density of 20mAg<sup>1</sup>. In situ Raman spectra were collected by a homemade coin cell setup under the combination of Renishaw inVia Raman spectroscope and the CV measurement with a scan rate of 1mVs<sup>1</sup> within a potential window of 02.0V. Optical photographs of electrodes soaked in an ethanol solution containing phenolphthalein were shot immediately after discharge/charge at different potentials under current density of 20mAg<sup>1</sup>. Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests were carried out with a constant dischargecharge current density of 20mAg<sup>1</sup> for 30min duration followed by a relation for 90min as one step within a potential range of 02.0V. The reaction kinetic information of diffusivity coefficient <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> can be calculated by analyzing the change of potential with time using the following Fick's second law formula:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="cey2479-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE--><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f588d821-1a98-48de-8b30-335a72a3e7fc/cey2479-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2479:cey2479-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/cey2479-math-0001.png">

                  </semantics></math> are the potential changes during the step response and current pulse process, respectively, which can be obtained from the GITT curves. For the full cell test, the cathode material was Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF), which was mixed with polyvinylidene fluoride and super P with a weight ratio of 8:1:1 in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone solvent. The slurry was coated onto Al foil and dried in a vacuum oven at 60C for 12h. The mass ratio of the cathodeanode was 1.5:1 in a typical full cell. The capacity of full cell was calculated based on anode active mass.&nbsp;The full cell was tested on a CT2001A battery test system (LAND; Wuhan LAND Electronic Co., Ltd.) in the voltage range of 1.03.3V.</div>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2479-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2479-sec-0080-title">3 RESULTS AND DISCUSSION</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2479-sec-0090">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2479-sec-0090-title">3.1 Material fabrication and characterization</h3>
            
            <p>As one of the five main plastics, polystyrene has been extensively used, giving rise to a massive accumulation of discarded waste and imposing an enormous environmental threat. Therefore, converting waste polystyrene plastics into valuable materials, such as carbon, has been proven to be one of the most effective ways.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0029_R_d170554616e769" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Sci Rep">29</a>-<a href="#cey2479-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0031_R_d170554616e769" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem Eng J">31</a></sup></span> Employing hyper-crosslinking reactions to prepare porous polymers/carbons is an efficient way to make high-value use of polystyrene plastic.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0032_R_d170554616e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - React Funct Polym">32</a></sup></span> According to our previous research, the resulting carbons generated from polystyrene are closely related to the crosslinking chemistry; for example, porous carbons with well-defined nanostructures can be prepared using carbonyl crosslinking chemistry,<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0033_R_d170554616e775" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nanoscale">33</a></sup></span> while the direct linking of pendant benzene rings produces low-porosity carbons with destroyed nanomorphology due to the carbonization-induced fusion effect.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0028_R_d170554616e778" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Carbon">28</a></sup></span> Therefore, there is a wealth of opportunity to merge the advantages of different crosslinking chemistry with synergistic effects for the development of advanced carbon materials. Motivated by such a design concept, TZ-PS and CO-PS nanospheres were used as precursors to prepare carbon anode materials for Na storage (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0001">1</a>). Specifically, monodisperse polystyrene spheres were prepared by microemulsion polymerization,<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0034_R_d170554616e785" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Sci Rep">34</a></sup></span> followed by respective hypercrosslinking and carbonization.</p>
            
            <p>Polystyrene spheres display well-defined spherical morphologies with smooth surfaces (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>), which were then subjected to triazine and carbonyl crosslinking (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>). The well-defined spherical morphologies retain for both TZ-PS and CO-PS (Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>), indicative of the occurrence of crosslinking within spheres rather than between spheres.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0034_R_d170554616e803" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Sci Rep">34</a>, <a href="#cey2479-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0035_R_d170554616e803" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J Funct Polym">35</a></sup></span> Such crosslinking can be further verified by FTIR spectroscopy spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>). For TZ-PS, additional peaks ascribed to the triazine stretching vibration appear at 1509 and 1384cm<sup>1</sup>,<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0036_R_d170554616e812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chem Eng J">36</a></sup></span> while the carbonyl peak at 1656cm<sup>1</sup> can be observed for CO-PS.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0033_R_d170554616e817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nanoscale">33</a></sup></span> As verified by N<sub>2</sub> adsorptiondesorption isotherms (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>), TZ-PS and CO-PS demonstrate <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> of 572 and 332m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup>, respectively, significantly higher than that of polystyrene nanospheres without crosslinking (23m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup>; Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>), further demonstrating the occurrence of hypercrosslinking reactions.</p>
            
            <p>To better understand the structural evolution during the carbonization process, CO-PS and TZ-PS pyrolyzed at different temperatures were characterized, and the schematic representation of their distinct morphology evolutions is summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0002">2A</a>. The SEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0002">2B</a>) show that the well-maintained spherical nanostructure can be always seen at 300C, 400C, 500C, 600C, and 800C when CO-PS is used as the carbon precursor, confirming the decisive role of CO in achieving good nanostructure inheritability during carbonization.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0034_R_d170554616e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Sci Rep">34</a>, <a href="#cey2479-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0037_R_d170554616e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem Commun">37</a>, <a href="#cey2479-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0038_R_d170554616e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J Mater Chem">38</a></sup></span> In contrast, TZ-PS gradually fails to maintain the monodisperse spherical morphology from 400C and fuses into the layered morphology completely at 600C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0002">2C</a>). From the TGA and differential thermal analysis results in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0002">2D</a>, the decomposition peak occurs at 430C for polystyrene nanospheres, whereas the pyrolysis peak of TZ-PS moves to a higher temperature of 446C. This result indicates that the triazine crosslinking postpones the polymeric chain scission/decomposition.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0039_R_d170554616e860" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Polym Degrad Stab">39</a>-<a href="#cey2479-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0041_R_d170554616e860" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ChemNanoMat">41</a></sup></span> From FTIR spectra, the triazine rings (1500 and 1376cm<sup>1</sup>) of TZ-PS pyrolysis at 300C become broader and weaker, probably due to the decomposition of the triazine bond (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0002">2E</a>). TZ-PS pyrolysis at 300C shows the decrease of <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> to 295m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0002">2F</a>), indicative of the reduction of micropores.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0041_R_d170554616e879" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ChemNanoMat">41</a></sup></span> When pyrolysis temperature reaches 600C, <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> further decreases to 5m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup> with the complete collapse of nanoporous structures and spherical morphology (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0002">2F</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>), in contrast to carbonyl-crosslinker that favors well morphology preservation with tremendous nanopores.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0042_R_d170554616e896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv Mater">42</a>, <a href="#cey2479-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0043_R_d170554616e896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J Am Chem Soc">43</a></sup></span> Taken together, it can be inferred that the decomposition preferentially occurs at triazine bond with decreased micropores, and thus polystyrene chains are not stable enough to maintain the spherical morphology, leading to the fusion of TZ-PS.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2479-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7196db28-4cf1-4404-b0cf-30988c23229d/cey2479-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7196db28-4cf1-4404-b0cf-30988c23229d/cey2479-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d7af8fc6-7062-488d-a6a9-8f1edea2b8eb/cey2479-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7196db28-4cf1-4404-b0cf-30988c23229d/cey2479-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2479-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.479" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) Morphology derivation illustration of CO-PS and TZ-PS as a function of pyrolysis temperature. SEM images of (B) CO-PS and (C) TZ-PS under different pyrolysis temperatures. (D) TG and DTG curves of polystyrene nanospheres and TZ-PS. (E) FTIR spectra and (F) N<sub>2</sub> adsorptiondesorption isotherms of TZ-PS under different pyrolysis temperatures.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The combination of two precursors offers an effective way to produce HHCs with simultaneous regulation of micropore and nanomorphology. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0003">3A</a>, the spherical morphology maintains at 300C for both TZ-PS and CO-PS. When the temperature rises to 400C, the nanospherical structure from CO-PS is wrapped with large layers derived from TZ-PS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0003">3B</a>). With increasing the temperature to 800C, the morphology of carbon spheres embedded into the&nbsp;layered carbon matrix remains well (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0003">3C</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8B</a>). Even if carbonization times were extended to 12h or reduced to 1h, such morphology still remains, indicative of the robust hybrid structure prepared by the in situ fusion and embedding strategy (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8A,C</a>). High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images exhibiting the interlayer spacings of HHC, CO-PS, and TZ-PS carbonized at 800C (the latter two are denoted as CO-PS-800 and TZ-PS-800, respectively) are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0003">3DF</a>. The microstructures of HHC include two different interlayer spacings, originating, from CO-PS and TZ-PS, respectively. Larger interlayer spacing from CO-PS-800 helps to reduce the diffusion barrier and provides more Na channels.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0044_R_d170554616e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Chem Eng J">44</a>-<a href="#cey2479-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0046_R_d170554616e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J Mater Chem A">46</a></sup></span> The corresponding selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns gradually increase from CO-PS-800, HHC, to TZ-PS-800 showing a similar trend of average interlayer spacing.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2479-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/354db37e-737c-4785-8857-4a5087014529/cey2479-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/354db37e-737c-4785-8857-4a5087014529/cey2479-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c1739a2c-ca56-477f-96f6-a11de5dccd99/cey2479-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/354db37e-737c-4785-8857-4a5087014529/cey2479-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2479-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.479" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of TZ-PS and CO-PS mixed at 1:1 mass ratio under pyrolysis temperatures of (A) 300C, (B) 400C, and (C) 800C. HRTEM and SAED images of (D) TZ-PS-800, (E) CO-PS-800, and (F) HHC. The interlayer spaces of these three samples are around 0.36, 0.37, 0.39, and 0.39nm in Areas 1, 3, 2, and 4, highlighting the morphology-guiding effects of TZ-PS and CO-PS, respectively.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>More detailed carbon framework microstructures were evaluated by X-ray diffraction (XRD) and Raman spectroscopy. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0004">4A</a>, the two peaks located at 23.224.5 and 44.3, respectively, indexed to (002) and (100) graphitic planes, emerge in HHC, TZ-PS-800, and CO-PS-800. The characteristic peaks are broader and weaker in CO-PS-800, while TZ-PS-800 has a relatively sharper and narrower peak with a higher angle, meaning the relatively more ordered structure with fewer defects. The peak position of HHC is located at 23.4 which is between that of TZ-PS-800 and CO-PS-800. The <i>d</i>-spacing value of (002) plane (<i>d</i><sub>002</sub>) can be calculated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0004">4C</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>). HHC shows <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> of 0.380nm, between 0.362nm of TZ-PS-800 and 0.383nm of CO-PS-800. The Raman spectra of these three samples show the typical D band at 1330cm<sup>1</sup> and G band at 1590cm<sup>1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0004">4B</a>), which are, respectively, associated with disordered-led C-bond or defect-induced D-band and the in-plane bond-stretching G-band.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0020_R_d170554616e996" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv Energy Mater">20</a></sup></span> The intensity ratio of the D peak to the G peak, <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value, of HHC is 2.67, showing a similar trend with <i>d</i><sub>002</sub>, between 2.53 of TZ-PS-800 and 2.79 of CO-PS-800 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0004">4C</a>), reflecting that HHC has more defects than TZ-PS-800 but less than CO-PS-800.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2479-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4f4c31c6-a0ad-40a3-8b92-8d7b23c33659/cey2479-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4f4c31c6-a0ad-40a3-8b92-8d7b23c33659/cey2479-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f5dbe369-a8fc-4b27-aefd-768e6e54d3e9/cey2479-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4f4c31c6-a0ad-40a3-8b92-8d7b23c33659/cey2479-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2479-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.479" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) XRD, (B) Raman spectra, (C) <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> and <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>, (D) N<sub>2</sub> adsorptiondesorption isotherms, (E) SAXS patterns, and (F) true density and closed pore volume of HHC, TZ-PS-800, and CO-PS-800.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The pore structures of these three samples were investigated by N<sub>2</sub> adsorptiondesorption isotherms, SAXS, and true density tests. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0004">4D</a> displays the typical N<sub>2</sub> adsorptiondesorption isotherms, and HHC bears an <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> of 7m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup>, lower than CO-PS-800 of 445m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup>, but higher than TZ-PS-800 of 2m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup>. It is found that the <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> of HHC can be further tailored by the carbonization time. For example, HHCs demonstrate monotonously decreased <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> from 16, 7 to 5m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup> upon increasing time from 1, 3 to 12h, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9A</a>). To embody the advantages of in situ fusion in HHC, HHC-MIX was prepared by physical mixing of TZ-PS-800 and CO-PS-800 in a mass of 1:1 ratio. <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> of HHC-MIX is much larger than that of HHC (141 vs. 7m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup>). Here, a plugged open hole effect coefficient (PHEC) is employed using the following formula to evaluate the synergistic effect:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="cey2479-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE--><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e7383537-1e3d-48a9-aab8-64d0c2e820fe/cey2479-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2479:cey2479-math-0006" display="block" displaystyle="true" location="graphic/cey2479-math-0006.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>PHEC</mi>
                                 <mo>=</mo>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                                 <mo></mo>
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>S</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>BET</mi>
                                          <mo>(</mo>
                                          <mi>sample</mi>
                                          <mo>)</mo>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>S</mi>
                                          <mrow>
                                             <mi>BET</mi>
                                             <mo>(</mo>
                                             <mi>TZ</mi>
                                             <mo></mo>
                                             <mi>PS</mi>
                                             <mo></mo>
                                             <mn>800</mn>
                                             <mo>)</mo>
                                          </mrow>
                                       </msub>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mrow>
                                             <mo>(</mo>
                                             <mn>1</mn>
                                             <mo></mo>
                                             <mi>P</mi>
                                          </mrow>
                                          <mrow>
                                             <mi>CO</mi>
                                             <mo></mo>
                                             <mi>PS</mi>
                                             <mo></mo>
                                             <mn>800</mn>
                                          </mrow>
                                       </msub>
                                       <mo>)</mo>
                                       <mo>+</mo>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>S</mi>
                                          <mrow>
                                             <mi>BET</mi>
                                             <mo>(</mo>
                                             <mi>CO</mi>
                                             <mo></mo>
                                             <mi>PS</mi>
                                             <mo></mo>
                                             <mn>800</mn>
                                             <mo>)</mo>
                                          </mrow>
                                       </msub>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>P</mi>
                                          <mrow>
                                             <mi>CO</mi>
                                             <mo></mo>
                                             <mi>PS</mi>
                                             <mo></mo>
                                             <mn>800</mn>
                                          </mrow>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </mfrac>
                                 <mo>,</mo>
                              </mrow>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $\mathrm{PHEC}=1-\frac{{S}_{\mathrm{BET}(\mathrm{sample})}}{{S}_{\mathrm{BET}(\mathrm{TZ}-\mathrm{PS}-800)}{(1-P}_{\mathrm{CO}-\mathrm{PS}-800})+{S}_{\mathrm{BET}(\mathrm{CO}-\mathrm{PS}-800)}{P}_{\mathrm{CO}-\mathrm{PS}-800}},$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where <i>P</i><sub>CO-PS-800</sub> is the CO-PS-800 ratio of sample and <i>S</i><sub>BET(sample)</sub> is the surface area of the composite. HHC demonstrates PHEC up to 0.97, far exceeding HHC-MIX of 0.37, indicative of intimate contact/interaction with reduced interfacial barrier via in situ fusing/embedding of polystyrene-based precursors.</div>
            
            <p>SAXS and true density tests are effective in analyzing the closed pore structure which is difficult to evaluate via the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption test.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0047_R_d170554616e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv Energy Mater">47</a>-<a href="#cey2479-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0049_R_d170554616e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Science">49</a></sup></span> SAXS patterns of these samples in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0004">4E</a> exhibit a plateau around the scattering vector (<i>Q</i>) of 0.1<sup>1</sup>, indicative of the presence of closed pores. Another essential metric for determining the closed-pore structure within a carbon material is the true density density.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0012_R_d170554616e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - SusMat">12</a></sup></span> HHC has a lower true density (2.10gcm<sup>3</sup>) than CO-PS-800 (2.18gcm<sup>3</sup>) and graphite (2.26gcm<sup>3</sup>) but higher than TZ-PS-800 (2.07gcm<sup>3</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0004">4F</a>). Accordingly, the calculated closed pore volume of HHC is 0.034cm<sup>3</sup>g<sup>1</sup>, between that of CO-PS-800 (0.016cm<sup>3</sup>g<sup>1</sup>) and TZ-PS-800 (0.041 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>1</sup>). Based on the formation mechanism of pyrolytic carbon, the decomposition of triazine moiety and the collapsing of nanoporous structures in TZ-PS may form some closed pores. Meanwhile, the closed pore volume of HHCs showcases an upward trend upon increasing the carbonization time (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9B</a>). Combined with the decrease in <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9A</a>) displayed above, it can be inferred that upon extending carbonization time, the open pores may transform to the closed ones in resulting HHCs. Our fabricated sample has a low external surface area and larger interlayer to minimize the initial irreversible Na loss associated with SEI formation&nbsp;and a high concentration of closed pores to maximize specific storage capacity. Therefore, the Na ion storage performances were investigated below.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2479-sec-0100">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2479-sec-0100-title">3.2 Na storage performances</h3>
            
            <p>The Na storage performances were tested in a half-cell configuration with Na metal as the counter/reference electrode. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0005">5A</a> shows the cycling performances at 20mAg<sup>1</sup> in the potential window from 0 to 2.0V. HHC has a better cycling performance with a charge capacity of 295.5mAhg<sup>1</sup> after 100 cycles, as compared with CO-PS-800 (103.5mAhg<sup>1</sup>) and TZ-PS-800 (147.3mAhg<sup>1</sup>). The first galvanostatic dischargecharge curves in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0005">5B</a> show that HHC exhibits an initial charge capacity of 279.3mAhg<sup>1</sup> with ICE of 70.2%, outperforming TZ-PS-800 (165.0mAhg<sup>1</sup> and 58.3%) and CO-PS-800 (132.1mAhg<sup>1</sup> and 46.0%). Experimentally, the dischargecharge curves can be divided into a high-potential sloping region (&gt;0.1V) related to the adsorption process on the surface and nanodomains/defects, and a low-potential plateau region associated with intercalation into graphitic layers and quasi-metallic Na nanocluster formation.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0012_R_d170554616e1249" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - SusMat">12</a>, <a href="#cey2479-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0050_R_d170554616e1249" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Nat Commun">50</a></sup></span> The percentages of plateau and slope capacities were further analyzed (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>). It can be seen that HHC shows higher discharge plateau capacity and the corresponding proportion (136.2mAhg<sup>1</sup> and 34.3% for first cycle) than CO-PS-800 (54.1mAhg<sup>1</sup> and 18.8% for first cycle) due to the presence of closed pores and fewer defects.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0028_R_d170554616e1260" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Carbon">28</a>, <a href="#cey2479-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0047_R_d170554616e1260" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv Energy Mater">47</a>, <a href="#cey2479-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0051_R_d170554616e1260" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv Energy Mater">51</a></sup></span> The higher capacity and ICE of HHC than TZ-PS-800 could be due to the larger average carbon interlayer space of HHC, which would result in effective Na<sup>+</sup> channels for intercalation and utilization of closed pores, in spite of slightly lower closed porosity of HHC than TZ-PS-800.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0004_R_d170554616e1265" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - New Carbon Mater">4</a></sup></span> With the carbonization time extended, the initial charge capacity and ICE increase, and HHC-12h shows the highest values of 336.6mAhg<sup>1</sup> and 73.7%, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>), due to the decrease in open pores and increase in closed pores (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>). It should be highlighted that under low carbonization temperature of no more than 1000C, the ICE over 70% for HHC is superior to most of the previously reported carbon materials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0005">5C</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>), and the ICE could be further boosted by surface coating, electrolyte optimization and the employment of additives in the future.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0052_R_d170554616e1283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Carbon">52</a>-<a href="#cey2479-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0054_R_d170554616e1283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Energy Environ Sci">54</a></sup></span> The rate capability was examined using stepwise current densities ranging from 0.05 to 2Ag<sup>1</sup> to evaluate the fast-charging characteristics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0005">5D</a>). With rich closed pores, fewer defects, and suitable interlayer space, the reversible capacities of HHC are 275.1, 278.2, 240.3, 185.0, 132.8 and 83.1mAhg<sup>1</sup> in the first cycle at each current density (50, 100, 200, 500, 1000 and 2000mAg<sup>1</sup>, respectively), outperforming those of TZ-PS-800 and CO-PS-800. Surprisingly, HHC also exhibits an outstanding capacity retention of 85% at 1000mAg<sup>1</sup> over 500 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0005">5E</a>), indicative of its good durability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2479-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/84f0e77d-d967-45b5-9e31-4517ee4c2130/cey2479-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/84f0e77d-d967-45b5-9e31-4517ee4c2130/cey2479-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/284d48d8-696f-4823-8423-4cc82226d014/cey2479-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/84f0e77d-d967-45b5-9e31-4517ee4c2130/cey2479-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2479-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.479" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) Cycling performance and (B) galvanostatic dischargecharge curves of the first cycle for HHC, TZ-PS-800, and CO-PS-800 at 20mAg<sup>1</sup>. (C) Comparison of the ICE and carbonization temperature of representative hard carbon anodes. (D) Rate performances at 50, 100, 200, 500, 1000, and 2000mAg<sup>1</sup> for HHC, TZ-PS-80, and CO-PS-800. (E) Long-cycle performance of HHC at 1000mAg<sup>1</sup>. The initial two cycles were performed at 50mAg<sup>1</sup>. The capacity in the <i>y</i>-axis labels in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0005">5</a> refers to the charge capacity.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The Na adsorptiondesorption characteristics were further analyzed by CV at a scan rate of 0.1mVs<sup>1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>). All three samples show a couple of redox peaks at 00.1V and irreversible cathodic peaks at 0.4V in the initial cycle, corresponding to the plateau region and the formation of the SEI layer, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0055_R_d170554616e1347" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - ACS Appl Mater Interfaces">55</a></sup></span> The CV curves at various scan rates from 0.1 to 1mVs<sup>1</sup> were used to estimate the diffusion-controlled and capacitive-dominated processes during the sodiation/desodiation process,&nbsp;in which the <i>b</i> value can be obtained (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> and Equation&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0056_R_d170554616e1361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - J Mater Chem A">56</a></sup></span> The calculated <i>b</i> value of HHC (0.701) is similar to that of TZ-PS-800 (0.696) and much nearer to 0.5 than that of CO-PS-800 (0.912), implying a more diffusion-controlled contribution in HHC than in CO-PS-800 (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>). Additionally, the Equation&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> can be employed to calculate the contributions of diffusion- and capacitive-type storage percentage.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0057_R_d170554616e1373" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - ACS Appl Mater Interfaces">57</a></sup></span> For instance, the diffusion contribution approaches 56% for HHC at 0.3mVs<sup>1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16A</a>). The diffusion contributions of these samples under various sweep rates are summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16B</a>. With increasing the scan rate, the diffusion contribution decreases and HHC always shows a higher diffusion contribution than TZ-PS-800 and CO-PS-800, indicative of the excellent ion diffusion process in the HHC electrode.</p>
            
            <p>To understand the systematic insights into the sodiation/desodiation mechanism and nanostructure changes of HHC electrode, in situ Raman spectra were performed. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0006">6A</a>, the peak positions of both D- and G-band witness a negative shift within the range from 2 to 0V in the discharge stage, suggesting partial ions intercalation into pseudographitic layers; their peak position of G-band recovers from 0 to 2V in the charging process, indicative of the desodiation with the reversible recovery of the structure.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0058_R_d170554616e1390" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - ACS Appl Mater Interfaces">58</a></sup></span> Additionally, the cycled HHC electrodes were put into ethanol solution containing phenolphthalein to prove the presence of quasi-metallic Na in the closed pores of HHC as the producer CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>ONa of Na and ethanol can make phenolphthalein solution red.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0012_R_d170554616e1397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - SusMat">12</a>, <a href="#cey2479-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0059_R_d170554616e1397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Adv Energy Mater">59</a></sup></span> Evidently, the color of the ethanol solution turns pink and gradually deep with the continuous discharge process, and the color becomes lighter within the charging process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0006">6B</a>), which verifies the existence of quasi-metallic Na in HHC and the sodiation/desodiation mechanism of the plateau-potential region based on closed pores filling.<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0060_R_d170554616e1404" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv Energy Mater">60</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2479-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/383328a0-6d98-4030-9561-485efac904b5/cey2479-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/383328a0-6d98-4030-9561-485efac904b5/cey2479-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f1967d39-e50e-4a00-a522-3c657d83a7b2/cey2479-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/383328a0-6d98-4030-9561-485efac904b5/cey2479-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2479-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.479" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Analysis of Na storage mechanism of HHC. (A) In situ Raman spectra of HHC electrode in a dischargecharge cycle. (B) The typical discharge/charge profile of the HHC electrode and the color changes of ethanol containing 1% phenolphthalein after reaction with HHC at different potentials. (C) Calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients for the sodiation/desodiation process by GITT test. (D) Scheme illustration of Na storage mechanism in HHC.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The GITT was performed to measure the diffusivity coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>), and the obtained GITT curves <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> were provided in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> and&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0006">6C</a>, respectively. The <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of all samples remains relatively stable with a potential higher than 0.1V during the sodiation process, corresponding to the adsorption-dominated Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior. After that, <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of HHC and TZ-PS-800 declines rapidly at first and rises later within the 0.10V, mainly ascribed to the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and nanoclusters formation in the closed pores, respectively. These results confirm the formation of low-defect, enriched closed pores in HHC derived from the TZ-PS-800, following the previously proposed adsorption-insertion-filling mechanism,<span><sup><a href="#cey2479-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0060_R_d170554616e1456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv Energy Mater">60</a>, <a href="#cey2479-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2479-bib-0061_R_d170554616e1456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Carbon">61</a></sup></span> as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0006">6D</a>, whereas the CO-PS-800 shows a very weak decline and rebound process, indicative of the few intercalations and Na nanocluster formation due to the abundant open pores and defects in CO-PS-800. Such porous nanostructures in HHC from CO-PS help to facilitate the ion diffusion capability, as can be reflected by higher <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of HHC than TZ-PS-800 in the high-potential sloping region (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2479-fig-0006">6C</a>). Meanwhile, HHC showcases lower charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) than TZ-PS-800 under various potentials via in situ EIS (Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>), further confirming the effective role of defects and larger interlayers derived from CO-PS-800.</p>
            
            <p>To highlight the synergistic structural advantage of HHC, it is compared with HHC-MIX (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>). The SEM image of HHC-MIX shows carbon nanospheres agglomerating together and being distributed outside the layered carbons (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20A</a>). In contrast, high-porosity carbon nanospheres are tightly embedded/anchored on the layered carbon via in situ fusion and embedding for HHC. Such structural differences are depicted in the illustration (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20B</a>). The charge capacity of HHC is higher than that of HHC-MIX at 2000mAg<sup>1</sup> (83.1 vs 55.2mAhg<sup>1</sup>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20D</a>). EIS reveals that HHC delivers smaller electrochemical resistance, implying that the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer in HHC is better than that of HHC-MIX (Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20C</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>). This disparity stems from the fact that HHC has a continuous entity and smooth ion transport channels by virtue of the in situ fusion and embedding strategy.</p>
            
            <p>Given the structural merits of HHC, full cells paired with NVPF (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>) cathode and HHC anode were assembled to demonstrate the feasibility for practical applications (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>). The full cell delivers a discharge capacity of 133.5mAhg<sup>1</sup> at 200mAg<sup>1</sup> after 500 cycles based on the mass of the HHC anode. Moreover, the proof-of-the-concept pouch cell is evaluated, delivering a capacity of 90.4mAhg<sup>1</sup> at 200mAg<sup>1</sup> and good cycling stability with capacity retention of 88.5% after 70 cycles. These outstanding performances demonstrate that the HHC is a promising anode for SIBs. To verify the generality of the in situ fusion and embedding strategy, waste polystyrene plastic was used to prepare HHC via the proposed triazine- and carbonyl-crosslinking process (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24A</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>). The as-acquired sample still exhibits excellent Na storage performances in half cell (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24B</a>). In this way, this approach offers an advanced and efficient reutilization approach for waste plastics.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2479-sec-0110">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2479-sec-0110-title">4 CONCLUSION</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a versatile design of HHCs from hypercrosslinking polystyrene bearing distinct molecular bridges at a low carbonization temperature of 800C was proposed. This is realized via an in situ fusion and embedding strategy, in which TZ-PS and CO-PS nanospheres were integrated during carbonization. The well-retained carbon spheres with suitable interlayer spacing derived from carbonyl crosslinking were embedded into layered carbon with fewer defects and richer closed pores from the triazine crosslinking. Therefore, HHCs exhibit synergistic Na ions storage behavior with simultaneous high ICE (70.2%), large charge capacity (279.3mAhg<sup>1</sup>), and long-term stability, which is much higher than those of CO-PS-800 and TZ-PS-800. The adsorption-insertion-filling Na storage mechanism of HHC has been proven and the excellent performances demonstrate the validity and rationality of the proposed HHC design. Together with the low carbonization temperature and low-cost waste polystyrene plastics, the present findings would open new avenues towards fabricating cost-effective hard carbon anode materials for accelerating the practical adoption of SIB technology.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2479-sec-0120-title"> ACKNOWLEDGMENTS</h2>

G2022KY0607, 23GH0202277).</p>
